Chapter 5. Decimal Fractions.
Colleagues! We have already studied ordinary fractions and have mastered
all the secrets of operations with ordinary fractions to perfection. Therefore, we understand that
calculations with ordinary fractions can be complex and cumbersome. Furthermore,
calculations with natural numbers can be performed "in columns," but for ordinary
fractions nothing similar exists. Therefore, in real-life calculations, not
all ordinary fractions are used, but only some of them. Which ones?

Definition. Ordinary fractions whose denominators contain the numbers
10, 100, 1000, 10000, 100000 and so on (that is, powers of 10) are called
decimal fractions.

Thus, a decimal fraction is an ordinary fraction whose denominator
7 13
is one of the numbers 10, 100, 1000 and so on. For example, — is a decimal fraction, while the fraction
10 20
is not decimal. Let us also note that the use of decimal fractions is very convenient from
the standpoint of the metric system of measurement. Indeed, since 1 cm = 10 mm, then 1 mm
1
constitutes one-tenth of a centimeter, that is, 1 mm = cm. Similarly, since 1 m =
10
1 1
100 cm, then 1 cm = m. Further, 1 km = 1000 m, therefore 1 m = km. The same
100 1000
1
situation applies to units for measuring mass: since 1 kg = 1000 g, then 1 g = kg. Thus, we see
1000
that in the metric system of measurement, some units of measurement are expressed through others precisely with
the help of decimal fractions. And once again let us emphasize that decimal fractions are a special
case of ordinary fractions, and not some "new" fractions. Therefore, with decimal
fractions one can perform the same operations as with ordinary fractions -
compare, apply the fundamental property of fractions, perform arithmetic operations.

For decimal fractions there exists a special system of notation. It was invented so
that all operations with fractions could be performed uniformly - "in columns." When
writing a decimal fraction, the whole part is separated from the fractional part by a comma, and
the fractional part is written after the comma, with the number of zeros in
the denominator of the ordinary fraction showing the number of digits after the comma in
the notation of the decimal fraction. Let us consider examples:

202

## Page 207

7
2 2, 7 - here after the comma there is one digit, since the denominator of the original fraction
10
equals 10
57
3 3, 57 - here after the comma there are two digits, since the denominator of the original fraction
100
equals 100
5757
57 57, 5757 - here after the comma there are four digits, since the denominator of the original
10000
fraction equals 10000
These fractions are read the same way as common fractions. For example, 2,7 - "two and seven
tenths", 3,57 - "three and fifty-seven hundredths", 57,5757 - "fifty-seven and five
thousand seven hundred fifty-seven ten-thousandths". If the whole part of a common
fraction is absent, then in the decimal fraction notation a zero is placed
3 29
before the comma. For example, 0, 3 , 0, 29 . These fractions are called: 0,3 - "zero and three
10 100
7
tenths", 0,29 - "zero and twenty-nine hundredths". And how do we write the fraction 5 as a
100
decimal fraction? The main rule when converting common fractions to
decimal fractions - the number of zeros in the denominator of the common fraction must be
equal to the number of digits after the comma in the decimal fraction notation. Therefore, when
7
writing the fraction 5 there must be two digits after the comma, however the numerator of this fraction
100
consists of only one digit. In such a case the missing places are replaced by the digit
7
zero, that is 5 5, 07 - "five and seven hundredths". Let's give more examples of similar
100
situations:
57
7 7, 0057 - "seven and fifty-seven ten-thousandths"
10000
1
0, 00001 - "zero and one hundred-thousandth"
100000
507
0, 000507 - "zero and five hundred seven millionths"
1000000

On the positional number system.
The decimal number system, in which natural numbers are written, is called positional. This means that in the notation of each number, the value of a digit depends on which place it is in, in what position the digit stands (hence the name comes - positional). Units of two adjacent places differ from each other by a factor of 10.
For example, the notation 57 means that the number has 7 units and 5 tens, that is 575 × 10 + 7×1.
Similarly, 234792 × 10000 + 3×1000 + 4×100 + 7×10 + 9×1. Thus, we write the number in powers of the number 10, that is, we show how many units, tens, hundreds, thousands, etc. the number contains. The digit 0 in the notation of a number shows the absence of units of the corresponding place, for example, 703097 × 10000 + 0×1000 + 3×100 + 0×10 + 9×1. All places have their names. So, from right to left, in ascending order, go units, tens, hundreds, thousands, tens of thousands, hundreds of thousands, millions, tens of millions, hundreds of millions, billions and so on.
207

## Page 212

Let's show that the method of writing decimal fractions is a natural generalization of the given method of writing natural numbers. Let's take an arbitrary decimal fraction and write it as a common fraction:
923067
0,123067
1000000
Let's write the numerator of this fraction according to the definition of writing natural numbers:
923067 9 × 100000 + 2×10000 + 3×1000 + 0×100 + 6×10 + 7×1
0,923067 
1000000 1000000
The rule for adding fractions with the same denominators allows us to write this fraction in the following form:
923067 9 × 100000 2 × 10000 3 × 1000 0 × 100 6 × 10 7 × 1
0,923067  + + + + +
1000000 1000000 1000000 1000000 1000000 1000000 1000000
Let's reduce each of these fractions by the power of 10 standing in the numerator:
923067 9 2 3 0 6 7
0,923067  + + + + + 
1000000 10 100 1000 10000 100000 1000000
9 × 0,1 + 2×0,01 + 3×0,001 + 0×0,0001 + 6×0,00001 + 7×0,000001.
9 2
So, we see that the fraction 0,923067 consists of fractions (nine tenths), (two hundredths),
10 100
3 0 6
(three thousandths), (zero ten-thousandths), (six hundred-thousandths),
1000 10000 100000
7
(7 millionths). Similarly, any decimal fraction can be written
1000000
as a sum of tenths, hundredths, thousandths, etc. It turns out that any decimal fraction -
just like a natural number - is written in a positional number system
by place values. And if in the whole part the place values are powers of the number 10, in the fractional part
1 1 1 1
the place values are the numbers , , and so on, that is, powers of the number . These place values
10 100 1000 10
have their natural names - from left to right, in decreasing order - tenths, hundredths,
thousandths, ten-thousandths, hundred-thousandths, millionths, ten-millionths,
hundred-millionths, billionths, and so on.

## Page 215

Comparing decimal fractions.
The advantages of working with decimal fractions are clearly visible already when comparing
decimal fractions. So, for comparing common fractions we had different methods:
comparing fractions with the same numerator, with the same denominator, by deficiency...
Let's propose a simple and clear algorithm for comparing decimal fractions. Let's consider examples.
Example 1. Compare 16.6278 and 27.899.
The given decimal fractions have different integer parts, and 16<27. Therefore,
16.6278<27.899.
Example 2. Compare 5.87 and 5.15.
The integer parts of these fractions are the same. Therefore, to compare these fractions, let's write
                87      15
them as common fractions (and we won't reduce!): 5— and 5—. Since the given fractions had
               100     100
the same number of digits after the decimal point, the fractions were "freely" reduced to a common
                           87   15
denominator. Now it's easy to see that 5— > 5—. So, 5.87 > 5.15.
                          100  100
Let's consider a more complex example.
Example 3. Compare 0.8976514 and 0.8976522.
These fractions have the same number of digits after the decimal point, so writing these fractions as
                    8976514      8976522
common fractions, we get fractions with the same denominators: ——————— and ———————.
                   10000000     10000000
Now it's sufficient to compare only the numerators of these fractions. And such "long" natural
numbers are convenient to compare by digits: we find (going from left to right) the first digit in
which the numbers differ – and the number is greater that has the larger
digit in this position: 8976514<8976522. So, 0.8976514 < 0.8976522.
In the two previous examples it was convenient for us to compare decimal fractions, since in the notation
of these fractions after the decimal point there was the same number of digits. And that means these fractions had
the same denominators. But how do we compare fractions that have a different number of digits after
the decimal point? Let's consider the following example.
211

## Page 216

Example 4. Compare 6.7898 and 6.7895341
We will reduce these fractions to a common denominator using the basic property of decimal fractions: if
we append (or remove) zeros at the end (right) of a decimal fraction, we
get a fraction equal to the given one. We append enough zeros at the end of the first fraction
so that both fractions have the same number of digits after the decimal point: 6.7898000 and 6.7895341.
Now we have obtained fractions with the same denominator, and, as in the previous example, we can
compare them by digits: 6.7898000 > 6.7895341. Therefore, 6.7898 > 6.7895341.
The examples considered allow us to formulate the following algorithm for comparing
decimal fractions.
1. If the integer parts of decimal fractions are different, then the fraction with the
larger integer part is greater.
2. To compare two decimal fractions with the same integer part, first
equalize the number of digits after the decimal point by appending zeros on the right.
3. Now compare decimal fractions by digits (going from left to right): the fraction
with the larger first non-matching digit after the decimal point is greater.
Let's use this purely formal rule to compare fractions 4.305629 and 4.30568.
We have: 4.305629 < 4.305680.

## Page 218

Operations with decimal fractions.
Addition and subtraction of decimal fractions.
Let's perform the addition of fractions 16.2 and 3.18. First, by the basic property of decimal
fractions, we'll reduce these fractions to a common denominator, that is, equalize the number of digits after
the decimal point: 16.2 + 3.18→16.20 + 3.18. Then we'll write the fractions as ordinary fractions and convert to
improper fractions:
20 18 1620 318 1938
16.2 + 3.18→16.20 + 3.18→16 + 3  +  →19.38
100 100 100 100 100
Similarly, we'll perform subtraction of fractions:
20 18 1620 318 1302
16.2 - 3.18→16.20 - 3.18→16 -  -  →13.02
100 100 100 100 100
Thus we see that addition and subtraction of decimal fractions reduces to addition
and subtraction of natural numbers. Indeed, after equalizing the number of decimal
places, we add two natural numbers, as if dropping the comma, and in the answer we
separate with a comma as many digits as there were in each of the fractions – in this example 2 digits.
And addition and subtraction of natural numbers is conveniently done "in columns". Therefore, with
decimal fractions these operations can also be done "in columns"! We propose the following
algorithm for addition and subtraction of decimal fractions.
1. Equalize the number of digits after the decimal point in the fractions.
2. Write the fractions one under the other so that the comma is under the comma.
3. Perform addition (or subtraction) of decimal fractions, paying no attention
to the comma.
4. Place the comma in the answer under the comma in the given fractions.
5. If after performing operations several zeros appear at the end of the fractional part,
then they should be crossed out.

Multiplication of decimal fractions.
Let's perform the multiplication of fractions 16.2 and 3.18. We'll convert these fractions to common fractions and write them as improper fractions:
2 162 18 318
16.2=16  , 3.18=3 
10 10 100 100
We'll multiply the fractions according to the rule for multiplying common fractions – "numerator times numerator, denominator times denominator". We get:
162 318 162 × 318
16.2 × 3.18= × =
10 100 10 × 100
We'll naturally multiply the numerators "in columns" and get that
51516
16.2 × 3.18=
1000
According to the rule for writing decimal fractions – the number of digits after the decimal point equals the number of zeros in the denominator – we'll write the answer as a decimal fraction. So, finally we get: 16.2 × 3.18=51.516. Note that in the first factor there was one digit after the decimal point, in the second – two, and in the product – three digits after the decimal point.

Let's consider another example: 0.015 × 0.0007. We'll perform analogous calculations: convert the fractions to common fractions, multiply them, and then write the resulting fraction as a decimal. We have:
15 7 15 × 7 105
0.015 × 0.0007= × = =0.0000105
1000 10000 1000 × 10000 10000000

Let's analyze this example and derive a general rule for multiplication. In the first factor there were 3 digits after the decimal point, in the second – 4. This means that in the denominator of the first fraction there are 3 zeros, and in the denominator of the second – 4. According to the rule for multiplying "round" numbers, the number of zeros is added, that is, in the denominator of the product there will be 3 + 4=7 zeros, and then – according to the rule for writing decimal fractions – in the answer there will be 7 digits after the decimal point. Thus we see that the number of decimal digits in the product is added from the number of decimal digits in each of the factors. Let's formulate the algorithm for multiplying decimal fractions.

1. Drop the decimal points in the factors and multiply the resulting natural numbers.
2. In the resulting product, separate with a decimal point from the right as many decimal digits as there are in both factors together.

Division of a decimal fraction by a natural number.

Multiplication of decimal fractions, as we have seen, is easily reduced to multiplication of natural numbers. With division – everything is much more complicated... Therefore, we will begin with division of a decimal fraction by a natural number and still try to draw an analogy with division of natural numbers "in columns."

Division of natural numbers is performed digit by digit: first the larger counting units are divided, and then consecutively – digit by digit – the smaller ones, while we consecutively express some digits through others: thousands – through hundreds, hundreds – through tens, tens are "broken down" into units. Since decimal fractions are also written by digits, we can generalize the principle of division of natural numbers for decimal fractions as well. Namely: we divide "in columns" as usual, digit by digit, and express some digits through others: units – through tenths, tenths are broken down into hundredths, hundredths – into thousandths, thousandths – into ten-thousandths and so on. In this case, if decimal digits turn out to be insufficient, then by the basic property of decimal fractions we can always append to the right as many digits as required.

All the above vague reasoning leads us to a simple algorithm for dividing a decimal fraction by a natural number. Let us formulate the algorithm for dividing a decimal fraction by a natural number.

1. Perform the division, paying no attention to the comma.
2. Place the comma in the answer at the moment when we begin to use digits in the dividend that are located after the comma.
3. If necessary, append to the right of the dividend as many zeros as needed.

Division of decimal fractions.
Now let's consider division by a decimal fraction. This case is easily reduced to
division by a natural number. Indeed, as we well know, the division operation can be
written not only using a "colon," but also using a fraction bar. For example,
0.3105
0.3105 ÷ 1.5
1.5
Now by the fundamental property of fractions, we multiply the numerator of this fraction by such a number that there are no commas in the denominator. In this case we need to multiply by 10. We have:
0.3105 0.3105 × 10 3.105
0.3105 ÷ 1.5   3.105 ÷ 15
1.5 1.5 × 10 15
Thus, we have reduced division by a decimal fraction to division of a decimal fraction by a natural number. Now it's not difficult to get the answer "in columns" (be sure to check "in columns"!). So, 0.3105 ÷ 1.53.105 ÷ 150.207. Note that it's not necessary to get rid of all commas, you only need to get rid of commas in the divisor-denominator.
Let's consider one more example: 0.003345 ÷ 0.00025. Again let's write the division using a fraction bar:
0.003345
0.003345 ÷ 0.00025
0.00025
Since there are five digits after the comma in the denominator, to get rid of the comma in the denominator, we need by the fundamental property of fractions to multiply both the numerator and denominator by a number with five zeros, that is by 100000. We have:
0.003345 0.003345 × 100000
0.003345 ÷ 0.00025 
0.00025 0.00025 × 100000
Now by the rule of multiplication by such numbers, we move the comma five places to the right in both the numerator and denominator:
0.003345 0.003345 × 100000 334.5
0.003345 ÷ 0.00025   334.5 ÷ 25
0.00025 0.00025 × 100000 25
So, we have again replaced division by a decimal fraction with division by a natural number, which can be carried out "in columns" (be sure to check!). Finally we get:
0.003345 ÷ 0.00025334.5 ÷ 2513.38
moved
Comparing the expressions 0.003345 ÷ 0.00025 and 334.5 ÷ 25, we see that we
to the right
the comma in the dividend and in the divisor by as many places
in the divisor
as there are after the comma
.
223

## Page 228

Let's formulate the algorithm for dividing decimal fractions.
1. You cannot divide by a decimal fraction!!!
2. To divide by a decimal fraction, you need to move the decimal point in both the dividend and divisor the same number of digits to the right as there are digits after the decimal point in the divisor.
3. Perform the division of a decimal fraction by a natural number.
4. Let's emphasize once more that you need to eliminate the decimal point only in the divisor, while the decimal point may remain in the dividend!

CLXXX. Two bear cubs had a kilogram piece of cheese. They broke it into two
unequal pieces. A fox decided to help them make the pieces equal. First she
bit off from the larger piece. Then she bit off from the smaller piece half of what

from the smaller piece, and from the smaller piece again half as much as from the larger piece. And finally, she
again bit off from the larger piece the same amount as from the smaller piece, and from the smaller piece half
as much as from the larger piece. After this the pieces became equal, and the mass of the smaller
of the original pieces decreased by half. How much cheese did the fox eat?

## Page 243

Chapter 6. Combinatorics.
6 × 1. A bush has 4 large branches, on each large branch there are 7 small twigs, and on each
small twig there are 9 leaves, and on each leaf sit 3 ladybugs. How many
ladybugs are sitting on the bush?
Combinatorics is a branch of mathematics in which problems are solved with the question "how many
ways exist?". In principle, any combinatorial problem can be solved by enumeration.
We will consider several simple problems and derive the basic laws of combinatorics.
Problem I. From city A to city B lead two roads, and from city B to city C - three roads.
Travel is only possible "from left to right" (see figure). How many ways exist to travel from
city A to city C?
1 I
A II C
B
2 III
Solution. First let's solve this problem by enumeration: list all possible routes
of movement from A to C. For this let's designate the roads, for example, as shown in the figure:
roads from A to B we'll number with Arabic numerals, and roads from B to C - with Roman numerals. If we
travel from A to B on road 1, then further from B to C we can travel on any of roads
I, II or III. Thus, we get routes 1-I, 1-II, 1-III. Similarly, if from A to B
we travel on road 2, then we get routes 2-I, 2-II, 2-III. So, in total we get 6 ways
to travel from city A to city C.
239

## Page 244

The enumeration in such problems can be formatted as a so-called tree of possible variants. In this problem, this tree will look like this:
×
1 2 road from A to B
I II III I II III road from B to C
Now let's solve the same problem under the condition that 8 roads lead from A to B, and 11 roads lead from B to C (draw a diagram of the roads!). It's clear that this problem can also be solved by enumeration, but listing all the variants here would be long and boring. Let's schematically depict the tree of possible variants:
×
…
1 2 3 7 8
…
… …
I II XI
I II XI
It's not difficult to see that for each of the 8 variants of the path from A to B, there exist 11 variants of the path from B to C, that is, in total 8 × 11 = 88 ways to travel from A to C.
Note that we obtained the answer by multiplication.

Let's modify the problem once more: suppose there are two roads from city A to city B, three roads from city B to city C, and 4 roads from city C to city D. You can only travel "from left to right". Let's count how many ways there are to travel from A to D.
a

A II
c
2 III
d
As before, let's denote the roads as shown in the figure. Let's construct a tree of possible variants. It's easy to see that there are two ways to travel from A to B; for each of these ways there are three ways to travel from B to C. Thus, there are already 2 × 3 = 6 ways to travel from A to C. And for each of these 6 ways there are 4 ways to travel from C to D. In total, 6 × 4 = 24 ways to travel from A to D. Note that we again obtained the answer using multiplication: 2 × 3 × 4 = 24.

And once again let's change the problem condition: suppose now the road scheme has the following form.
B
C
A D
E
Travel, as usual, is only possible "from left to right". Let's count how many ways exist to travel from A to D. Here all routes are divided into two non-intersecting cases: either the route passes through B and C, or through E. The number of routes passing through B and C equals 2 × 2×312 (why? explain!); the number of routes passing through E equals 2 × 36 (why? explain!). Since traveling from A to D is possible either only through B and C, or only through E, the number of routes equals 12 + 618.

Problem II. How many three-digit numbers exist where all digits are even?

Solution. There are five even digits in total - these are 0, 2, 4, 6, 8. From these digits we need to first choose the first digit of the three-digit number. This can be done in 4 ways, since the digit 0 cannot be in the first position. Now for each choice of the first digit, the second digit can be chosen in 5 ways, and for each choice of the second digit, the third digit can also be chosen in 5 ways. Therefore, a three-digit number can be written with even digits in 4 × 5×5100 ways. Show what the possibility tree looks like for this problem.

Problem III. A tourism company plans to visit four cities in Italy: Venice, Rome, Florence and Naples. How many variants of such a route exist?

Solution. Let's denote the cities by their first letters. Then each route can be "encoded" with four letters. For example, the sequence РНФВ denotes the route Rome - Naples - Florence - Venice. Thus, we need to describe all sequences of four letters В, Р, Ф and Н. Any of the four letters can be in the first position, that is, there are four variants for filling the first position. Since we have already used one letter, there are only three variants for the second position. Now we have already used two letters, so for the third position there are only two variants. And finally, for the fourth position only one variant remains. Let's clarify our reasoning with a concrete example. Suppose, for example, the first city is Rome, that is, Р is in the first position. Then we choose the second city from only three cities - Venice, Florence and Naples. Suppose we chose Venice, that is, the letter В is in the second position of our sequence. Then we choose the third city from only two cities - Florence and Naples. If we, for example, chose Florence, then the fourth city will unambiguously be Naples. Thus, we get the sequence РВФН. By constructing a tree of possible variants (definitely do this!), it's not difficult to see that in total there exist 4 × 3×2 × 124 variants of the route.

Problem IV. A) In how many ways can a class of 30 people choose a class president and their deputy? B) In how many ways can a class of thirty people choose two duty students?

Solution. A) The class president can be chosen in 30 ways. After choosing the class president, we choose their deputy from the remaining 29 people, so for each choice of class president there are 29 ways to choose their deputy. Thus (draw a tree of possible variants!), we get 29 × 30 = 870 ways.

B) At first glance, this is the same problem: the first duty student can be chosen in 30 ways, the second in 29 ways, so it seems there are 29 × 30 = 870 ways to choose two duty students. It turns out that this is an incorrect solution and wrong answer. And not without reason did the ancient Greeks say: everything is understood through comparison!

Suppose there is a boy Lyova and a boy Vasya in the class. If we choose a class president and their deputy, then we can choose Lyova as president and Vasya as his deputy, or conversely - choose Vasya as president and Lyova as his deputy. And these are different cases! But if we choose two duty students, then we don't care in what order we name them - Vasya and Lyova or Lyova and Vasya. Thus, we counted this pair of duty students twice - but we should have counted it once. And so we counted each pair of duty students twice instead of once.

Therefore, to get the correct answer, we must divide 870 by half. So, the answer is: 29 × 30 ÷ 2 = 435.

Remark. Let's introduce some terminology that is convenient when solving combinatorial problems. Namely, we will say that in problem A) we choose an ordered pair: the class president is in the first position, and their deputy is in the second. And it matters to us who is in which position. And if we swap the first and second positions in this pair, we get a different case of choosing a class president and their deputy. In part B) we choose an unordered pair: we need to choose two people, but it doesn't matter to us who is in the first position and who is in the second. And if we swap the first and second positions in this pair, we get the same pair. Moreover, from each unordered pair we can get two ordered pairs, so the number of ordered pairs is twice as large as the number of unordered pairs.

Note that in combinatorics problems, not only pairs are considered, but also ordered and unordered sets of a larger number of elements. As an example, let's consider the following problem.

244

## Page 249

Problem V. A) Uncle Chernomor chooses three warriors for night duty. The duty is organized as follows: the first warrior takes duty at midnight, the second – at 2 AM, and the third – at 4 AM. In how many ways can Uncle Chernomor organize the duty? B) Due to the complication of the foreign policy situation, the duty schedule has changed: now all three warriors together guard Buyan Island all night. In how many ways can Uncle Chernomor organize the duty now?

Solution. A) The first warrior can be chosen in 33 ways. After that, we choose the second warrior from the remaining 32 warriors, so this choice can be made in 32 ways. For the third warrior, there are now only 31 options remaining. Thus, the number of ways to organize the duty equals 33 × 32 × 31 = 32736. Note that in this problem it matters in what order the warriors go on duty, that is, here we are considering ordered triples of warriors. B) But in this problem the warriors are on duty not in turn, but all together. And when Uncle Chernomor assigns warriors to duty, it doesn't matter in what order he names them. Thus, we need to find the number of unordered triples of warriors. Let's use the result from problem A). The number of ordered triples equals 33 × 32 × 31. However, with such counting, we counted each triple of warriors several times. Indeed, let's denote three warriors as A, B and C. By simple enumeration we can see that they can be ordered in 6 different ways (be sure to write out all variants!). So, we counted each unordered triple of warriors 6 times. Therefore, to get the correct answer we must divide the number 33 × 32 × 31 by 6. Thus, the number of ways to organize the duty equals 33 × 32 × 31 ÷ 6 = 5456.

When solving combinatorial problems it is very important to determine what sets - ordered or unordered - need to be counted!

Problem VI. There are 10 airports in a country. A permanent airline is established between any two airports. How many airlines are there in total in the country?
Solution. From each airport, 9 airlines depart - to all other airports. There are 10 airports in total, from each 9 airlines depart, resulting in a total of 9 × 1090 airlines. But with this counting method, we counted each airline twice. Indeed, we counted the airline between city A and city B twice: when we counted airlines departing from A, and when we counted airlines departing from B. Therefore, the number of airlines equals 9 × 10 ÷ 245. This solution method is called double counting.

Chapter 7. Divisibility tests.
Additional pages.
The proof of the divisibility test for 9 given in chapter 2 using examples of two- and three-digit numbers is not a proof in the strict sense of the word, since we considered only two particular cases and made a generalization to all numbers. In order to conduct a proof for any three-digit number, let's introduce a letter notation for three-digit numbers. We will denote a three-digit number as abc̄, where a, b, c are digits (that is, they can take values 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8 or 9), and the line above means that we are using decimal notation of the number. Thus, abc̄ = a×100+b×10+c×1. And now let's repeat the same reasoning that we conducted for specific numbers. Namely, let's represent the number abc̄ in the following form:
abc̄ = a×100+b×10+c = a×(99 + 1)+b×(9 + 1)+c = a×99+a+b×9+b+c = (a×99+b×9)+ (a+b+c)
                                                    ↑                    ↑
                                              divisible by 9      sum of digits
Thus, any three-digit number can be represented as the sum of two terms, the first of which is always divisible by 9, and the second is the sum of digits of the original number.
Consequently, when the sum of digits of the original number is divisible by 9, then the number itself is divisible by 9.
If the sum of digits of the number is not divisible by 9, then the number itself is not divisible by 9.

7 × 1. Prove in general form the divisibility test for 9 for four-digit numbers.
7 × 2. Prove in general form for three-digit numbers that a number and the sum of its digits give the same remainders when divided by 9.
7 × 3. Prove in general form that the difference between a three-digit number and the number written in reverse order is divisible by 99.
7 × 4. In the decimal notation of a four-digit number, the first digit coincides with the last, and the second with the third. Prove in general form that this number is divisible by 11.
7 × 5. Prove in general form that the difference between a five-digit number and the sum of its digits is divisible by 9.
7 × 6. In a four-digit number, the first digit coincides with the third, and the second with the fourth. Prove in general form that this number is divisible by 101.
7 × 7. Prove in general form that the sum of a four-digit number and the number written in reverse order is divisible by 11.
247

## Page 252

Exotic divisibility tests.
Let's also consider several exotic divisibility tests. First, let's prove
the divisibility test for 11. For this, note that the numbers 99, 9999, 999999, 99999999 and
so on -- numbers whose decimal representation uses an even number of
nines -- are divisible by 11 (why? check it!). We will prove the divisibility test for 11 using
the example of six-digit numbers. Let's write an arbitrary six-digit number as abcdef, where
a, b, c, d, e and f are digits, and the overline means that we use the decimal representation
of the number. Let's represent the number abcdef in the following form:
abcdef = a×100000+b×10000+c×1000+d×100+e×10+ f =
= (a×100000+b×10000)+(c×1000+d×100)+(e×10+ f ×1) =
= (a×10+b)×10000+(c×10+d)×100+(e×10+ f ) = ab×(9999 + 1)+cd×(99 + 1)+ef =
= ab×9999+ab+cd×99+cd +ef = ab×9999+cd×99 + (ab+cd+ef)
                                 \_________________/
                                  divisible by 11
Thus, we have represented any six-digit number as the sum of two
terms, the first of which is always divisible by 11, and the second is obtained from the original number
in the following way: we split the decimal representation of the original number into two-digit numbers,
going from right to left, and added the resulting two-digit numbers. If the resulting number is divisible
by 11, then the original number is also divisible by 11, and conversely: if the resulting number is not divisible by 11,
then the original number is not divisible either. In the general case, the rule is the same. We only note that
if the original number has an odd number of digits in its decimal representation, then the leftmost number
in the indicated partition will be single-digit. Let's consider how the divisibility test
for 11 works on a concrete example. Take the number 135795. Let's split its decimal representation into
blocks of two digits, going from right to left: 13|57|95. Let's add the resulting two-digit numbers:
13 + 57 + 95 = 165. Apply the divisibility test for 11 to the number 165 again. Split its
decimal representation into blocks - 1|65 - and find the sum: 1 + 65 = 66 = 6 × 11. So, 66 is divisible
by 11. Therefore, 165 is also divisible by 11. Consequently, 135795 is also divisible by 11.

7 × 8. Prove this divisibility test for 11 for three-digit and five-digit numbers.

248

## Page 253

For those who are familiar with negative numbers, we will prove another divisibility test for 11. It is based on the fact that the numbers 11, 1001, 100001, etc. and the numbers 99, 9999, 999999, etc. are divisible by 11 (why? check it!). We represent an arbitrary six-digit number abcdef in the following form:

abcdef = a×100000+b×10000+c×1000+d×100+e×10+ f ×1 =
= a×(100001 - 1)+b×(9999 + 1)+c×(1001 - 1)+d×(99 + 1)+e×(11 - 1)+ f =
= a×100001-a+b×9999+b+c×1001-c+d×99+d+e×11-e+ f =
= (a×100001+b×9999+c×1001+d×99+e×11)+ ( f -e+d-c+b-a)
        divisible by 11                    alternating sum of digits

Thus, we can represent any six-digit number as the sum of two terms, the first of which is always divisible by 11, and the second we will call the alternating sum of the digits of the number: digits standing in odd positions (counting from the right) enter this sum with a "+" sign, and digits standing in even positions (counting from the right) - with a "-" sign. Therefore, a number is divisible by 11 if and only if the alternating sum of its digits is divisible by 11. Let's consider an example. Take two numbers: 847095 and 135795. The alternating sums of digits for these numbers are equal to 5 - 9+0 - 7+4 - 8=-15 and 5 - 9+7 - 5+3 - 1=0 respectively. -15 is not divisible by 11, so 847095 is not divisible by 11. And the number 0 is divisible by 11, from which it follows that 135795 is divisible by 11.

**7 × 9.** Prove both divisibility tests for 11 for four-digit numbers.

Let's prove the divisibility test for 7. For this, we note that the number 1001 is divisible by 7: 1001=7 × 11 × 13. Let's consider an arbitrary six-digit number abcdef and represent it in the following form:

abcdef = a×100000+b×10000+c×1000+d×100+e×10+ f ×1 =
= (a×100000+b×10000+c×1000)+(d×100+e×10+ f ×1) =
= (a×100+b×10+c)×1000+(d×100+e×10+ f ×1) = abc×(1001 - 1)+def = abc×1001+def -abc,
        abc                     def

where the number abc×1001 is divisible by 7. Therefore, the number abcdef is divisible by 7 if and only if def -abc is divisible by 7. Thus, to check whether a number of at most six digits is divisible by 7, we need to divide it into blocks of three digits, going from right to left, and form the difference of the resulting numbers. Note that the second number can be two-digit or one-digit. For example, let's investigate the number 87318. We divide it into blocks: 87|318. Their difference is 318 - 87=231=33 × 7 is divisible by 7. Therefore, 87318 is also divisible by 7.

249

## Page 254

7 × 10. Formulate and prove a divisibility test for 7 for nine-digit numbers.
Hint: 999999 is divisible by 7.
7 × 11. Formulate and prove divisibility tests for 27 and 37. Illustrate these
tests with examples. Hint: 27 × 37 = 999, 1000 - 1.
Euclidean algorithm for finding GCD.
Let number d be a common divisor of numbers a and b. Then the difference of these numbers
is also divisible by d. Therefore, d is a common divisor of numbers a-b and b. Conversely:
let d be a common divisor of numbers a-b and b. Then the sum of these numbers (a-b)+b = a
is also divisible by d, that is, d is a common divisor of numbers a and b. Thus, the common divisors
of the pair of numbers a, b and the pair of numbers a-b, b are the same. In particular, GCD(a,b) = GCD(a-b,b).
This simple observation forms the basis of another method for finding GCD. This method does not
use factorization and is called the Euclidean algorithm. Let's find, for example,
GCD(765,136). As we showed above, we can replace the pair of numbers 765, 136 with the pair of numbers
765 - 136, 136. We can repeat this operation until subtraction is no longer possible.
It's easy to verify that 765 = 136 × 5 + 85. Therefore,
GCD(765,136) = GCD(765 - 136,136) = GCD(765 - 2×136,136) = ...
... = GCD(765 - 5×136,136) = GCD(85,136)
Thus, we replaced the number 765 with the remainder from dividing this number by 136. Now
let's divide 136 by 85 with remainder: 136 = 85 × 1 + 51. Therefore,
GCD(765,136) = GCD(85,136) = GCD(85,51). Further, 85 = 51 × 1 + 34, from which
GCD(765,136) = GCD(85,136) = GCD(85,51) = GCD(34,51). Further, 51 = 34 × 1 + 17, so
GCD(765,136) = GCD(85,136) = GCD(85,51) = GCD(34,51) = GCD(34,17). Again let's divide 34
by 17 with remainder: 34 = 17 × 2 + 0. So, the remainder equals zero, that is, 34 is divisible by 17 and
GCD(34,17) = 17. The Euclidean algorithm is written as follows:
765 = 136 × 5 + 85
136 = 85 × 1 + 51
85 = 51 × 1 + 34
51 = 34 × 1 + 17
34 = 17 × 2 + 0
Thus, the greatest common divisor of two numbers equals the last non-zero remainder in
the Euclidean algorithm.
7 × 12. Using the Euclidean algorithm, find the GCD of the following numbers:
A) 6457 and 658 B) 65864 and 5758 C) 2435 and 2436 D) 674585 and 71
E) 8417479 and 457 F) 1943 and 13479 G) 5589 and 2452 H) 8693 and 16101
250
